امر ہے اور اس ضعف فی الاصالہ کو ظاہر کرنے کی خاطر اس کو اصل مطلق سے جدا گانہ پیش کیا گیا ہے ۔ وہ اما الاب ابہ باقی رہا آپ کا اشتباہ تو وہ صحیح نہیں ہے کیونکہ اب اپنے باپ پر وجود یعنی موجود ہونے میں مبتنی ہے لیکن ابوۃ اور اصالة للولد میں مبتنی نہیں ہے لہذا یہ مانحن فیہ سے نہیں ہے۔ وعن الثاني خلاصہ یہ کہ قیاس کو سبب قریب پر قیاس کرنا مع الفارق ہے لان السبب القريب کیونکہ سبب قریب وہ ہوتا ہے جو فرع میں موثر ہو اور اس کی طرف منفی ہو اور سبب بعید کا اثر فرع تک نہیں پہنچتا بلکہ اسی سبب قریب تک پہنچتا ہے جو درمیان میں واسطہ کا کام دے رہا ہے اور یہی سبب قریب مؤثر في الفرع ہوتا لہذا بنسبت بعید کے یہی سبب قریب معنی سببیہ اور اصالہ میں اولیٰ اور اقوی ہے بنسبت بعید کے بخلاف قیاس کے کہ یہ حکم فرع کے لئے سرے سے مثبت ہی نہیں ہے پھر ہم اس کو سبب قریب کیسے قرار دے سکتے ہیں اور اولیٰ فی معنی السبیة کیسے کہہ سکتے ہیں در حقیقت حکم فرع مستند الى النص یا الی الاجماع ہوتا ہے اور قیاس صرف مظہر ہے۔ وعن الثالث حاصل جواب یہ کہ سائل نے جو اولویۃ بعض اقسام من البعض ہر تقسیم میں لازم قرار دی ہے یہ کلیہ غلط ہے ۔ ہر تقسیم میں اس کا لزوم ہم تسلیم نہیں کرتے۔ ماہیات حقیقیہ کی تقسیم جو الی الانواع والافراد ہوتی ہے اس تقسیم میں اولیہ بعض اقسام علی البعض کیسے متصور ہو سکتی جب کہ یہ ضابطہ مسلمہ عند المحققین ہے کہ لا تشکیک فی الماہیات تقسیم حیوان الى الانسان والفرس میں تشکیک کہاں ہے۔ ولو سلم اگر غیر محقق قول کے لحاظ سے ہر قسم کی تشکیک کو تسلیم کر بھی کیا جاوے تو پھر اس کی طرف اشارہ اور تنبیہ کے لزوم کو ہم تسلیم نہیں کرتے یعنی اس اولویت و عدم او لویہ کی طرف اشاره کرنا واجب و لازم نہیں ہے غایہ ما فی الباب جواز ہے یعنی جائز ہے کہ اشارہ کیا جائے اور نہ کیا جائے تو کوئی حرج نہیں ہے ۔ و عن الرابع جو تغییر من الخصوص الى العموم میں کیا گیا ہے کہ صورۃ اخری میں حکم ثابت بالقیاس ہوگا اگر یہ مراد ہے کہ تقریہ اور اثبات حکم فی الانواع باعتبار الواقع ہو گا تو تغییر کا انحصا ہم اس میں تسلیم نہیں کرتے اور ہم نہیں مانتے کہ اس کے بدوں تغیر ممتنع ہے ۔ اگر تقریر اور ثبوت حکم بحسب علمنا مراد ہو تو پھر تغیر کا یہ مفہوم صحیح ہے لیکن یہ اس کا متقاضی نہیں ہے کہ حکم کا اسناد اور نفس الامر کے لحاظ سے قیاس کی طرف کیا جائے کہ اس کو اصل مطلق تسلیم کرنا لازم ہو جاے و عن الخامس بعد تسليم ما ذكر اشارة الى قاله البعض من ان الاجماع قد يكون بلا سبب دار و ذلك بان يخلق الله تعالى فيهم العلم الضرورى فيوفقهم للصواب ان الاجماع انما يحتاج يعنى اگر تسلیم کر لیں کہ اجماع محتاج الی الاصل ہے تو اس اصل و سند کی طرف اپنے وجود اور تحقیق میں محتاج ہوتا ہے لیکن دلالت علی الحکم میں محتاج الی السند نہیں ہوتا بلکہ اس دلالہ میں وہ درجہ اصالہ رکھتا ہے اور مستدل بالاجماع مفتقر الی السنہ نہیں ہوتا بخلاف قیاس کے کہ اس میں دال علی الحکم نص ہوتی ہے قیاس صرف مظہر ہوتا ہے اور قیاس کے ساتھ استدلال ممکن ہی نہیں جب تک کہ اصول تلہ میں سے حقیقہ
It is a necessary matter, so it is essential that in every division, the weak division is separated and presented apart from the strong divisions, like here qiyas is presented separately with the fourth principle. For example, in the division of kalima, it is said: 'al-kalimatu qismani: ismun wa fi'lun, wa al-qismu al-thalithu huwa al-huruf' because the particle is weak relative to the remaining divisions, but that was not done. So it was necessary that the seventh principle, al-miqas, should not have been presented separately and individually. Fourth: the change of ruling in the effect of qiyas, which is mentioned that it changes the ruling from particular to general—this is not possible except with affirmation (taqrir): it is not possible unless it establishes the ruling in another form besides that specific form, because the change from particular to general requires that. When qiyas establishes the ruling in another form, the principle becomes absolute for the ruling, and that is the meaning of absolute origination (al-asalat al-mutlaqa). Fifth: indeed consensus also needs a proof, whether from the Book or from the Sunnah, just as qiyas needed the Sunnah; then consensus would not be an absolute source either. And the answer to the first: Our intent in not calling qiyas an absolute source is not that the knowledge of its being a branch is included in the concept of 'asl'. Therefore, the questioner's statement 'innahu la ya'qi al-asl al-awwal' becomes futile, because the questioner has presented this concept of 'asl', and we do not affirm that branch-ness or its absence is included in the concept of 'asl'. Rather, our intent is that the word 'asl' is applied equivocally (bi al-tashkik), meaning that its individuals differ in strength and weakness in the truth of the meaning of 'asl'. The individual of 'asl' that is independent in the meaning of origination and does not need anything else in the branch's dependence on it—like the Book—is stronger in origination compared to that 'asl' which is based on something else in the meaning of origination, in such a way that its branch is actually based on that other thing—like qiyas, because its branch, i.e., the ruling, is actually based on its source (makhadh). So qiyas is weak in the meaning of origination. And the weaker cannot be included among the absolute principles that are perfect in origination. This is clear, and it is due to this weakness in origination that it is presented separately from the absolute principle. As for your confusion about the father and son, it is not correct, because the son depends on the father in existence, but fatherhood and being an origin for the son are not dependent; hence this is not from our topic. And concerning the second: The summary is that comparing qiyas to a close cause (sabab qarib) is an invalid analogy (ma'a al-fariq) because the close cause is one that is effective in the branch and attributed to it, while the remote cause's effect does not reach the branch but reaches the close cause which acts as an intermediary, and it is the close cause that is effective in the branch. Therefore, compared to the remote, this close cause is prior and stronger in the meaning of causality and origination. Unlike qiyas, which does not establish the ruling for the branch at all; then how can we consider it a close cause and claim priority in causality? In reality, the ruling of the branch is attributed to the text or to consensus, and qiyas is only a manifestation (muzhir). And concerning the third: The gist of the answer is that the questioner's assumption of the priority of some divisions over others being necessary in every division is a false generalization. We do not accept its necessitation in every division. In the division of real essences (mahiyat haqiqiyya) into species and individuals, how can priority of some divisions over others be conceived, given the established rule among verifiers that there is no equivocation (la tashkik) in essences? In the division of animal into human and horse, where is equivocation? And even if we concede equivocation in every division according to the unverified statement, then we do not accept the necessity of indicating and pointing to it. That is, pointing to this priority or non-priority is not obligatory (wajib) or necessary; the utmost in the matter is permissibility (jawaz), meaning it is permissible to point to it or not, and there is no harm. And concerning the fourth: Regarding the change from particular to general, which is that the ruling in another form is established by qiyas: if what is meant is that the affirmation and establishment of the ruling in the types is according to reality (bi i'tibar al-waqi'), then we do not accept the dependence of change on this, and we do not believe that change is impossible without it. If what is meant is the affirmation and establishment of the ruling according to our knowledge, then this meaning of change is correct, but it does not require that the attribution of the ruling and the reality itself (nafs al-amr) is made to qiyas such that it becomes necessary to consider it an absolute source. And concerning the fifth: After conceding what was mentioned, there is an indication to what some have said, that consensus may be without cause (sabab) and that such consensus occurs by Allah creating necessary knowledge in them, thus guiding them to the truth; consensus indeed needs [a source]. Meaning if we concede that consensus needs a source, then it needs that source in its existence and verification, but in indicating the ruling it does not need a source; rather, in that indication it has a degree of origination, and the one who argues by consensus is not in need of the Sunnah, unlike qiyas, where the indicator of the ruling is the text, and qiyas is only a manifestation. Argument by qiyas is not possible unless from its principles (usulihi) the reality (haqiqa).
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi usul commentary responding to five objections against treating qiyas (analogy) as an absolute source (asl mutlak) in deriving rulings. The author addresses each objection systematically, clarifying the Hanafi position that qiyas is indeed a source of law but weak in origination compared to the Quran and Sunnah. 

The first objection argues that if qiyas is separated in division, it implies it is not an absolute source. The answer explains that the word 'asl' is used equivocally (tashkik); qiyas is an 'asl' but weak because its branch (the ruling) depends on a text, while the Quran is strong and independent. The father-son analogy is distinguished. 

The second objection likens qiyas to a close cause (sabab qarib). The reply shows that qiyas differs because the close cause directly affects the branch, whereas qiyas only manifests the ruling from a text; the ruling is actually attributed to the original text or consensus.

The third objection claims that in any division, the weak part must be pointed out. The author denies this rule, especially for real essences (mahiyat) where no equivocation exists; even if conceded, indicating priority is permissible, not obligatory.

The fourth objection involves qiyas changing a ruling from particular to general. The author distinguishes between real change (which qiyas does not effect) and change in our knowledge (which is acceptable but does not attribute the ruling to qiyas absolutely).

The fifth objection argues that consensus also needs a proof like qiyas, so it too would not be absolute. The refutation clarifies that consensus needs a source for its existence but not for its indication; it is independent in indicating the ruling, unlike qiyas which always relies on a text.

Throughout, the commentary follows the Hanafi school's precision, emphasizing that qiyas is a valid secondary source but not of the same rank as primary texts or consensus.

Key Terms
اصل مطلق (asl mutlak): An absolute source of law that is independent and does not require another source for its authority; here applied to the Quran and Sunnah.
تشکیک (tashkik): Equivocal application of a term to multiple individuals with varying degrees of intensity; here 'asl' applies to both strong (Quran) and weak (qiyas) sources.
سبب قریب (sabab qarib): A direct cause that produces an effect in a branch; distinguished from qiyas which does not directly cause the ruling.
ماهیت حقیقیہ (mahiyat haqiqiyya): Real essences, which are divided into species and individuals without equivocation (tashkik).
مظہر (muzhir): A manifestation or indicator of a ruling; qiyas is described as a muzhir because it shows the ruling derived from a text.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response to the first objection, why is qiyas not considered an absolute source (asl mutlak) even though it is called an 'asl'?
A) Because qiyas is not mentioned in the Quran or Sunnah.
B) Because the term 'asl' is applied equivocally, and qiyas is weak in the meaning of origination.
C) Because qiyas changes rulings from particular to general.
D) Because qiyas depends on consensus, which is not a primary source.

Key Takeaway
Qiyas is a valid but dependent source; its authority is not absolute like the Quran and Sunnah.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate
12 min
Qiyas Close Cause Priority and Objections
Responds to objections on qiyas and ijma

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze objections to qiyas concerning cause priority, division priority, need for change, and ijma's role, and apply the Hanafi responses in evaluating analogical reasoning.

Core Idea
The close cause is prior in qiyas; qiyas only manifests rulings, and objections about division priority, change, and ijma's source are refuted.

This passage from a Hanafi usul commentary tackles several objections raised against the role of cause in qiyas. It begins by establishing the priority of the close cause, then addresses objections regarding priority in divisions, the necessity of change, and the nature of ijma. Understanding these responses is key to correctly applying qiyas as a manifestation rather than a creative source.

Guided Path
1
Identify the close vs distant cause
Recognize that the close cause directly affects the branch, while the distant cause only reaches through the close cause.
This sets the foundation for causality.
2
Explain why close cause is prior
Because it is effective in the branch and original in causality, unlike the distant cause.
Priority is based on directness.
3
Distinguish qiyas from cause
Qiyas does not establish the ruling; it only manifests a ruling from nass or ijma.
This defines the scope of qiyas.
4
Analyze the third objection
The opponent claimed priority of parts is necessary in every division. Hanafi response: real essences (e.g., animal) have no hierarchy, and even if priority existed, it need not be indicated.
Reference to 'la tashkik fi al-mahiyyat'.
5
Evaluate the fourth objection
The opponent claimed qiyas requires change from specific to general. Hanafi response: change is not needed in actual attribution, only in our knowledge.
Distinction between reality and epistemology.
6
Assess the fifth objection
The opponent argued ijma needs a source. Hanafi response: ijma is independent in indicating the ruling and adds certainty (qat'i) even if the source is zanni, unlike qiyas which reduces to zanni.
Ijma's authority is sui generis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
کیونکہ سبب قریب وہ ہوتا ہے جو فرع میں موثر ہو اور اس کی طرف منفی ہو اور سبب بعید کا اثر فرع تک نہیں پہنچتا بلکہ اسی سبب قریب تک پہنچتا ہے جو درمیان میں واسطہ کا کام دے رہا ہے اور یہی سبب قریب مؤثر في الفرع ہوتا لہذا بنسبت بعید کے یہی سبب قریب معنی سببیہ اور اصالہ میں اولیٰ اور اقوی ہے بنسبت بعید کے بخلاف قیاس کے کہ یہ حکم فرع کے لئے سرے سے مثبت ہی نہیں ہے پھر ہم اس کو سبب قریب کیسے قرار دے سکتے ہیں اور اولیٰ فی معنی السبیة کیسے کہہ سکتے ہیں در حقیقت حکم فرع مستند الى النص یا الی الاجماع ہوتا ہے اور قیاس صرف مظہر ہے۔
وعن الثالث حاصل جواب یہ کہ سائل نے جو اولویۃ بعض اقسام من البعض ہر تقسیم میں لازم قرار دی ہے یہ کلیہ غلط ہے ۔ ہر تقسیم میں اس کا لزوم ہم تسلیم نہیں کرتے۔ ماہیات حقیقیہ کی تقسیم جو الی الانواع والافراد ہوتی ہے اس تقسیم میں اولیہ بعض اقسام علی البعض کیسے متصور ہو سکتی جب کہ یہ ضابطہ مسلمہ عند المحققین ہے کہ لا تشکیک فی الماہیات تقسیم حیوان الى الانسان والفرس میں تشکیک کہاں ہے۔ ولو سلم اگر غیر محقق قول کے لحاظ سے ہر قسم کی تشکیک کو تسلیم کر بھی لیا جاوے تو پھر اس کی طرف اشارہ اور تنبیہ کے لزوم کو ہم تسلیم نہیں کرتے یعنی اس اولویت و عدم او لویہ کی طرف اشاره کرنا واجب و لازم نہیں ہے غایہ ما فی الباب جواز ہے یعنی جائز ہے کہ اشارہ کیا جائے اور نہ کیا جائے تو کوئی حرج نہیں ہے ۔ و عن الرابع جو تغییر من الخصوص الى العموم میں کیا گیا ہے کہ صورۃ اخری میں حکم ثابت بالقیاس ہوگا اگر یہ مراد ہے کہ تقریہ اور اثبات حکم فی الانواع باعتبار الواقع ہو گا تو تغییر کا انحصا ہم اس میں تسلیم نہیں کرتے اور ہم نہیں مانتے کہ اس کے بدوں تغیر ممتنع ہے ۔ اگر تقریر اور ثبوت حکم بحسب علمنا مراد ہو تو پھر تغیر کا یہ مفہوم صحیح ہے لیکن یہ اس کا متقاضی نہیں ہے کہ حکم کا اسناد اور نفس الامر کے لحاظ سے قیاس کی طرف کیا جائے کہ اس کو اصل مطلق تسلیم کرنا لازم ہو جاے و عن الخامس بعد تسليم ما ذكر اشارة الى قاله البعض من ان الاجماع قد يكون بلا سبب دار و ذلك بان يخلق الله تعالى فيهم العلم الضرورى فيوفقهم للصواب ان الاجماع انما يحتاج يعنى اگر تسلیم کر لیں کہ اجماع محتاج الی الاصل ہے تو اس اصل و سند کی طرف اپنے وجود اور تحقیق میں محتاج ہوتا ہے لیکن دلالت علی الحکم میں محتاج الی السند نہیں ہوتا بلکہ اس دلالہ میں وہ درجہ اصالہ رکھتا ہے اور مستدل بالاجماع مفتقر الی السنہ نہیں ہوتا بخلاف قیاس کے کہ اس میں دال علی الحکم نص ہوتی ہے قیاس صرف مظہر ہوتا ہے اور قیاس کے ساتھ استدلال ممکن ہی نہیں جب تک کہ اصول تلہ میں سے حقیقہ
و قد يجاب اعتراض خامس کا یہ دوسرا جواب ہے حاصل اس کا یہ ہے کہ اجماع ایک ایسے امر زائد کو ثابت کرتا ہے جس کو سند ثابت نہیں کرتی وہ قطعیہ حکم ہے یعنی اجماع کا حکم قطعی ہو جاتا ہے اور بسا اوقات سند ظنی ہوتی ہے بخلاف قیاس کے کہ وہ کسی زیادۃ علی السند کا مفید نہیں ہوتا بلکہ الٹا مفید نقصان ہو جاتا ہے کہ حکم اصل قطعی ہوتا ہے اور حکم قیاس ظنی ہوتا ہے۔
Because the close cause (sabab qarib) is what is effective in the branch (far') and is attributed to it, and the distant cause's effect does not reach the branch but rather reaches that close cause which acts as an intermediary. And this close cause is effective in the branch. Therefore, compared to the distant cause, this close cause is prior and stronger in meaning of causality and originality. This is unlike qiyas, which does not establish the ruling for the branch at all, so how can we consider it a close cause and say it is prior in causality? In reality, the ruling of the branch is based on text (nass) or consensus (ijma), and qiyas is merely a manifestation (muzhir). Regarding the third objection: the summary of the answer is that the questioner made the priority (awlawiyya) of some parts over others necessary in every division; this universal statement is false. We do not accept its necessity in every division. In the division of real essences (mahiyyat haqiqiyya) into types and individuals, how can priority be conceived when it is an established principle among verifiers that there is no doubt (la tashkik) in essences? For example, in the division of animal into human and horse, where is doubt? Even if, based on an unverified statement, one concedes the possibility of priority in every division, we do not accept the necessity of indicating or alerting to it. i.e., indicating this priority or lack thereof is not obligatory; at most it is permissible. For the fourth objection: regarding the change from specific to general, if it is intended that the establishment of the ruling in the types actually occurs, then we do not accept the need for change, and we do not hold that without change it is impossible. If by establishment and proof of ruling according to our knowledge is meant, then this meaning of change is correct, but it does not necessitate that the ruling be attributed to qiyas in reality, such that it must be accepted as an absolute source (asl mutlaq). For the fifth objection: after conceding what was mentioned, an allusion to what some have said that ijma may be without a cause (sabab) that revolves, and that Allah creates necessary knowledge in them (the mujtahids) to guide them to the truth. Meaning: if we accept that ijma needs a source (asl), it is in need of that source in its existence and verification, but in terms of indicating the ruling, it is not in need of a source; rather in that indication it has the degree of originality (asala). And the one who argues by ijma is not in need of the sunna, unlike qiyas, where the indicator of ruling is the text, and qiyas is merely a manifestation, and argumentation with qiyas is not possible unless one considers one of its principles (usul) and a derived cause ('illa) from that principle. And the second answer to the fifth objection: the summary is that ijma establishes an additional matter that the source does not establish: it becomes a definitive (qat'i) ruling, while sometimes the source is probable (zanni). Unlike qiyas, which does not provide an addition to the source; rather it results in a deficiency: the original ruling is definitive, while the qiyas ruling is probable.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage is a commentary defending the Hanafi position on the role of cause (sabab) in qiyas and the distinct nature of ijma. The commentator responds to several objections. First, he argues that the close cause (sabab qarib) is the one directly effective in the branch, while the distant cause's effect stops at the close cause. Thus, in terms of causality and originality, the close cause is prior and stronger. Qiyas, however, does not establish the ruling; it merely manifests a ruling already present in the text or consensus. Then he addresses the third objection: the claim that priority of one division over another is necessary in every division is false—especially in real essences where there is no hierarchy. Even if such priority existed, it need not be explicitly indicated. Fourth objection: the change from specific to general in qiyas is not necessary unless it refers to our knowledge, not the actual attribution of the ruling to qiyas. Fifth objection: even if ijma requires an original source, it does not rely on that source for indication of the ruling; it is authoritative in itself. The second answer clarifies that ijma adds certainty to what may be a probable source, whereas qiyas cannot add such certainty and even reduces it to probability.

Key Terms
سبب قریب (sabab qarib): The close cause that directly affects the branch in qiyas and is the immediate reason for the ruling.
سبب بعید (sabab ba'id): The distant cause whose effect does not reach the branch directly but only through the close cause.
قیاس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning that extends a ruling from an original case to a branch case based on a shared cause; it merely manifests the ruling, not creates it.
اجماع (ijma): Consensus of mujtahids that establishes a ruling definitively, sometimes adding certainty beyond the original source.
لا تشکیک فی الماہیات (la tashkik fi al-mahiyyat): There is no doubt or hierarchy in real essences when dividing into types and individuals; all individuals share the essence equally.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the close cause (sabab qarib) considered stronger than the distant cause in qiyas?
A) Because it is directly from the Quran or Sunnah.
B) Because it has a direct effect on the branch and is prior in causality and originality.
C) Because the distant cause has no effect at all on the ruling.
D) Because qiyas itself is an independent source of rulings.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, qiyas only manifests rulings, and the close cause is the operative factor; objections based on universal priority or dependency of ijma are refuted.

Defending Istinbāṭ and Qiyās Against the Objection of Superfluity
Defends necessity of istinbāṭ and qiyās via taqdīr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the objection against the necessity of istinbāṭ and qiyās, and explain the first Hanafi response using taqdīr (ellipsis).

Core Idea
Every act has a ruling, but not all are explicitly stated — hence istinbāṭ and qiyās are needed.

A fundamental objection in usul al-fiqh is that if every human action already has a ruling from the Lawgiver attached to its specific evidence, then why bother with derived methods like istinbāṭ and qiyās? In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi response. The text provides two answers, the first relying on the concept of ellipsis (taqdīr). We'll analyse the reasoning and see how it defends the necessity of ijtihād.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Objection
The objection states that if all rulings are already linked to their evidence, then istinbāṭ and qiyās are superfluous. Understand the logical structure: if every act has a ruling, and that ruling is known from its evidence, what need is there to derive or analogize? This objection must be answered to justify the science of usul al-fiqh.
The objection is a rational challenge, not attributed to a specific school.
2
Recognise the First Answer (Taqdīr)
The text says there is an ellipsis (taqdīr) in the statement. The original intended meaning is not that every ruling is explicitly stated, but that some rulings are explicitly stated, and from those, others are derived and analogized. The phrase 'li yastanbiṭ' (so that it may be derived) is a reason for an implied thing: the explicit rulings are the basis for derivation.
Taqdīr is a common Hanafi hermeneutical tool for interpreting texts where the literal wording requires an implied element to be understood correctly.
3
Appreciate the Second Answer (Cut Off)
The text begins a second answer: the objection arises from a misunderstanding of the phrase. Although the answer is incomplete, it suggests that the objection wrongly assumes that 'every act has a ruling' means all rulings are known in detail, whereas in reality the rulings are attached in principle but require extraction. This second answer complements the first by addressing the conceptual confusion.
The second answer is cut off due to page break; future lessons may complete it.

Source Text

And the verification in this place: the gist of the verification is that Allah, exalted, did not create man in vain or purposelessly such that no ruling attaches to his actions. Rather, for every action of man there is some ruling from the Lawgiver—of the types of obligation, recommendation, prohibition, dislike, etc.—and this ruling is suspended and linked to its specific evidence, so that from it, when needed, it may be derived, and analogies may be drawn from that ruling to what is appropriate to it. An objection arises: when every action of man has some ruling from the Lawgiver attached to it and is linked to its evidence, then all rulings have been obtained; so what need is there for istinbāṭ (derivation) and qiyās (analogy)? One answer given is that there is an ellipsis (taqdīr) in the phrase, and 'li yastanbiṭ' is a reason for an implicit thing; the original is: 'Some of those rulings are explicitly stated; from its evidence it is derived when needed, and what is appropriate to that explicitly stated ruling is analogized to it.' A second answer is that the basis of the objection is that from this phrase it is misunderstood that...
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage addresses a rational objection to the methodology of usul al-fiqh. The objection arises from the statement that every human action has a ruling from the Lawgiver attached to its specific evidence; if so, all rulings are already known, so why do we need the processes of istinbāṭ (derivation) and qiyās (analogical deduction)? The text provides two answers. The first answer posits that there is an ellipsis (taqdīr) in the statement: what is meant is that some rulings are explicitly stated in the sources, while others are derived from their evidence when needed, and then analogies are made from those explicitly stated rulings to other similar cases. Thus, derivation and analogy are needed for rulings not explicitly stated. The second answer is cut off, but it suggests that the objection arises from a misunderstanding of the phrase—likely that the phrase does not imply that all rulings are already known in detail, but rather that they are attached in principle to their evidence, yet the actual extraction requires effort. The Hanafi position emphasizes that while the sources contain all rulings in principle, human intellect and methodology are required to extract and extend them via qiyās and istihsān. This is a foundational argument for the necessity of ijtihād.

Key Terms
استنباط (istinbāṭ): Derivation of a ruling from its specific evidence (dalīl) when needed.
قياس (qiyās): Analogical deduction where a ruling of an explicit case is extended to a similar case due to a common cause (ʿilla).
تقدير (taqdīr): Ellipsis or implied word in the statement that must be supplied to understand the intended meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first answer in the passage, why is istinbāṭ and qiyās still needed despite every act having a ruling?
A) Because the rulings are only for prophets, not common people.
B) Because the phrase has an ellipsis: only some rulings are explicitly stated, others need derivation.
C) Because qiyās is stronger than nass.
D) Because the rulings are not actually attached to acts.

Key Takeaway
The necessity of istinbāṭ and qiyās is defended through taqdīr (ellipsis), which clarifies that not all rulings are explicitly known, thereby requiring intellectual effort.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning

Universal Premises in Qiyas: Categorical and Conditional Syllogisms
Explaining universal propositions as premises in qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how universal propositions function as major premises in categorical syllogisms and as universal implications in conditional syllogisms, and apply the concept of tadammun to connect specific rulings to general usul principles.

Core Idea
Universal propositions in Usul al-Fiqh are the major premises of qiyas, whether categorical or conditional, and they include specific principles by inclusiveness.

In this passage, the author of al-Tawdih explains that the major premise in a categorical syllogism (qiyas hamli iftira'i) used in fiqh is a universal proposition from Usul al-Fiqh. Similarly, in a conditional syllogism (qiyas sharti istithna'i), the universal implication (mulazama kulliyya) is also a universal proposition from Usul al-Fiqh. Importantly, these specific universal propositions may not be explicitly stated in the books of usul, but they are included (by tadammun) in more general principles that are stated.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two forms of qiyas
Recognize that the passage describes two logical forms: categorical syllogism (qiyas hamli iftira'i) and conditional syllogism (qiyas sharti istithna'i). The first uses a major premise; the second uses a universal implication.
Categorical syllogism is the standard form of qiyas al-illa in Hanafi usul.
2
Locate the major premise or universal implication
In the example given, the statement 'Every ruling whose establishment qiyas indicates is established' is the major premise of the categorical syllogism. In the conditional, 'Whenever qiyas indicates the establishment of this ruling, then this ruling is established' is the universal implication.
Both are universal propositions (qadaya kulliyya) from Usul al-Fiqh.
3
Understand the role of tadammun (inclusiveness)
The passage notes that the exact universal implication may not be found verbatim in usul books, but it is contained within a more general universal principle mentioned in usul. For example, the specific 'Whenever qiyas indicates obligation, obligation is established' is included in the general 'Whenever qiyas indicates the establishment of every ruling that has this status, this ruling is established.'
Tadammun is a form of implicit inclusion, like a specific case falling under a general rule.
4
Apply the concept to a concrete example
Take a ruling like 'permissibility of a sale with deferred payment validated by qiyas'. The universal implication 'Whenever qiyas indicates permissibility, it is permissible' is derived from the general usul principle 'Whenever qiyas indicates a ruling, that ruling is established.'
This shows how furu' (branches) are linked to usul (principles).
5
Check the certainty level
In Hanafi usul, these universal propositions are considered qat'i (definitive) until subject to takhsis. The student should note that the implication is binding unless a specific exception is proven.
This is a key difference from the Shafi'i school, which often treats such general statements as zanni.

The author said in al-Tawdih: 'And he negated with the mentioned propositions...' meaning Usul al-Fiqh was knowledge of universal propositions by which one proximately reaches fiqh. By these propositions we mean those that are one of the two premises, the minor and major, of the proof of fiqh issues, and this premise is the major premise of the proof. For example, when you argue on an issue using a categorical syllogism (qiyas hamli iftira'i) in the first figure, its major necessarily is among these universal propositions. Our statement: 'This ruling is established' is like a claim. 'Because it is a ruling whose establishment qiyas indicates, and every ruling whose establishment qiyas indicates is established' — this is the proof of the mentioned claim, and the second premise which is the major is a universal proposition of Usul al-Fiqh. 'And when you argue...' meaning when you argue on fiqh issues using a conditional syllogism (qiyas sharti istithna'i) which depends on universal implications (mulazimat kulliyyah), and from the existence of the antecedent the existence of the consequent is proven, then the universal implications are from these same propositions of Usul al-Fiqh. Like our statement: 'This ruling is established' is a claim, its proof is: 'For whenever qiyas indicates the establishment of this ruling, then this ruling is established' — this is a universal implication (mulazama kulliyya) which is among the propositions of Usul al-Fiqh. But 'qiyas indicates the establishment of this ruling' is the exception of the existence of the antecedent, so the result is the existence of the consequent, i.e., 'this ruling is established'. And its title: It is possible that this universal proposition which we use as a major or as a universal implication is not mentioned in the exact wording in the issues of Usul al-Fiqh, but it is necessarily included in some universal proposition that is mentioned in Usul al-Fiqh. For example: 'Whenever qiyas indicates obligation in a disputed case, obligation is established therein' — this premise is not mentioned in Usul al-Fiqh in this detail, but it is included in this universal implication: 'Whenever qiyas indicates the establishment of every ruling that has this status, this ruling is established'. Now obligation, prohibition, etc. are particular rulings included in 'every ruling'. So the detail of this universal is: 'Whenever qiyas indicates existence, obligation is established; whenever qiyas indicates permissibility, permissibility is established.' So the implication which we made one premise of the proof is mentioned in the issues of Usul al-Fiqh by way of inclusiveness (bi-tariq al-tadammun). The commentator said in al-Talwih: 'His statement: meaning by universal propositions...' Know that a complete compound (murakkab tam) which is susceptible to truth and falsehood has different names according to different considerations and aspects. The commentator mentioned them, saying: From the aspect of including a judgment (hukm), whether negative or affirmative, it is called a proposition (qadiyya). From the aspect of being susceptible to truth and falsehood, it is called a report (khabar). From the aspect of conveying a judgment, it is called an informing (ikhbar). From the aspect of being one of the two premises of a proof, it is called a premise (muqaddima). From the aspect of being sought through proof, it is called the sought (matlub). From the aspect of being obtained from the proof, it is called a conclusion (natija). From the aspect of being located in knowledge and questioned about, it is called a problem (mas'ala). So the essence is one, and these different expressions and names, which have reached seven, are due to different considerations. And the subject of judgment (al-mahkum 'alayhi) in a proposition is called the subject (mawdu'), and the predicate (al-mahkum bihi) is called the predicate (mahmul) — this is in a categorical proposition (qadiyya hamliyya). If it is conditional (shartiyya), then they are called antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tali) respectively. And in the sought (matlub) and conclusion (natija), the subject is called the minor term (asghar) and the predicate the major term (akbar). And the proof (dalil) necessarily consists of two...
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage elaborates on how universal propositions (qadaya kulliyyah) from Usul al-Fiqh function in legal reasoning. The author explains that when a jurist constructs a categorical syllogism (e.g. in the first figure of qiyas hamli iftira'i), the major premise is a universal proposition from Usul al-Fiqh. Similarly, in a conditional syllogism (qiyas sharti istithna'i) based on universal implications, the conditional statement (mulazama) is also a universal qadiyya from Usul al-Fiqh. An example is given: 'Whenever qiyas indicates the establishment of a ruling, that ruling is established.' The text also addresses that these universal propositions may not be explicitly found in Usul al-Fiqh in the exact form used, but they are included implicitly in more general principles (through tadammun, inclusiveness). The commentator from al-Talwih then provides a classification of a complete compound proposition (murakkab tam) according to seven different aspects: as a proposition (qadiyya), report (khabar), informing (ikhbar), premise (muqaddima), sought (matlub), conclusion (natija), and problem (mas'ala). In a categorical proposition, the subject is called 'mawdu' and predicate 'mahmul'; in a conditional, they are 'muqaddam' and 'tali'. In the context of a proof (dalil), the subject of the sought/conclusion is the minor term (asghar) and the predicate the major term (akbar). This demonstrates the logical structure underlying Hanafi usul al-fiqh.

Key Terms
قضية كلية (qadiyya kulliyya): Universal proposition that serves as a major premise or conditional in qiyas
قياس حملي افترائي (qiyas hamli iftira'i): Categorical syllogism in the first figure, used to derive rulings by analogy
قياس شرطي استثنائي (qiyas sharti istithna'i): Conditional syllogism in which the antecedent is affirmed to deduce the consequent
ملازمة كلية (mulazama kulliyya): Universal implication that forms the major premise of a conditional syllogism
مركب تام (murakkab tam): Complete compound proposition that is either true or false

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when a jurist uses a categorical syllogism in the first figure, the major premise is:
A) A specific fiqh ruling
B) A universal proposition from Usul al-Fiqh
C) A verse from the Quran
D) The minor premise

Key Takeaway
The major premises of qiyas, whether categorical or conditional, are universal propositions from Usul al-Fiqh, and they include specific rulings by inclusiveness (tadammun).

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate
8 min
The Seven Aspects of a Complete Compound Proposition in Usul al-Fiqh
Classifying complete propositions by seven aspects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the seven names of a complete compound proposition (murakkab tam) based on different aspects, and distinguish between subject/predicate in categorical propositions and antecedent/consequent in conditional ones.

Core Idea
A single complete proposition has seven names depending on how it is considered: as a judgment, a report, an informing, a premise, a sought, a conclusion, or a problem.

The commentator in al-Talwih expands on the nature of universal propositions by explaining that a complete compound (murakkab tam) that can be true or false is called by different names according to different considerations. These names include qadiyya, khabar, ikhbar, muqaddima, matlub, natija, and mas'ala. The passage also clarifies the terminology for subject and predicate in categorical versus conditional propositions, and for the minor and major terms in a proof.

Guided Path
1
Understand the term 'murakkab tam'
A complete compound proposition is a meaningful statement that can be true or false. It is the building block of legal reasoning.
It contrasts with murakkab naqis (incomplete compound) like a phrase.
2
List the seven names and their aspects
From the passage, the seven names are: qadiyya (proposition) from aspect of containing a judgment; khabar (report) from aspect of being susceptible to truth/falsehood; ikhbar (informing) from aspect of conveying a judgment; muqaddima (premise) from aspect of being a part of a proof; matlub (sought) from aspect of being sought through proof; natija (conclusion) from aspect of being obtained from proof; mas'ala (problem) from aspect of being located in knowledge and questioned about.
The essence (dhat) is one; names differ by consideration.
3
Distinguish between categorical and conditional propositions
In a categorical proposition, the subject of judgment is called mawdu' and the predicate mahmul. In a conditional proposition, they are called muqaddam (antecedent) and tali (consequent).
In the sought/conclusion, the subject is asghar (minor) and predicate akbar (major).
4
Apply the classification to a legal statement
Take the statement 'Wudu is obligatory for prayer'. As a qadiyya, it contains a judgment; as a khabar, it can be true or false; as a muqaddima, it can be used in a proof; as a matlub, it is what we seek to prove; etc.
This shows how the same sentence functions differently in legal reasoning.

The author said in al-Tawdih: 'And he negated with the mentioned propositions...' meaning Usul al-Fiqh was knowledge of universal propositions by which one proximately reaches fiqh. By these propositions we mean those that are one of the two premises, the minor and major, of the proof of fiqh issues, and this premise is the major premise of the proof. For example, when you argue on an issue using a categorical syllogism (qiyas hamli iftira'i) in the first figure, its major necessarily is among these universal propositions. Our statement: 'This ruling is established' is like a claim. 'Because it is a ruling whose establishment qiyas indicates, and every ruling whose establishment qiyas indicates is established' — this is the proof of the mentioned claim, and the second premise which is the major is a universal proposition of Usul al-Fiqh. 'And when you argue...' meaning when you argue on fiqh issues using a conditional syllogism (qiyas sharti istithna'i) which depends on universal implications (mulazimat kulliyyah), and from the existence of the antecedent the existence of the consequent is proven, then the universal implications are from these same propositions of Usul al-Fiqh. Like our statement: 'This ruling is established' is a claim, its proof is: 'For whenever qiyas indicates the establishment of this ruling, then this ruling is established' — this is a universal implication (mulazama kulliyya) which is among the propositions of Usul al-Fiqh. But 'qiyas indicates the establishment of this ruling' is the exception of the existence of the antecedent, so the result is the existence of the consequent, i.e., 'this ruling is established'. And its title: It is possible that this universal proposition which we use as a major or as a universal implication is not mentioned in the exact wording in the issues of Usul al-Fiqh, but it is necessarily included in some universal proposition that is mentioned in Usul al-Fiqh. For example: 'Whenever qiyas indicates obligation in a disputed case, obligation is established therein' — this premise is not mentioned in Usul al-Fiqh in this detail, but it is included in this universal implication: 'Whenever qiyas indicates the establishment of every ruling that has this status, this ruling is established'. Now obligation, prohibition, etc. are particular rulings included in 'every ruling'. So the detail of this universal is: 'Whenever qiyas indicates existence, obligation is established; whenever qiyas indicates permissibility, permissibility is established.' So the implication which we made one premise of the proof is mentioned in the issues of Usul al-Fiqh by way of inclusiveness (bi-tariq al-tadammun). The commentator said in al-Talwih: 'His statement: meaning by universal propositions...' Know that a complete compound (murakkab tam) which is susceptible to truth and falsehood has different names according to different considerations and aspects. The commentator mentioned them, saying: From the aspect of including a judgment (hukm), whether negative or affirmative, it is called a proposition (qadiyya). From the aspect of being susceptible to truth and falsehood, it is called a report (khabar). From the aspect of conveying a judgment, it is called an informing (ikhbar). From the aspect of being one of the two premises of a proof, it is called a premise (muqaddima). From the aspect of being sought through proof, it is called the sought (matlub). From the aspect of being obtained from the proof, it is called a conclusion (natija). From the aspect of being located in knowledge and questioned about, it is called a problem (mas'ala). So the essence is one, and these different expressions and names, which have reached seven, are due to different considerations. And the subject of judgment (al-mahkum 'alayhi) in a proposition is called the subject (mawdu'), and the predicate (al-mahkum bihi) is called the predicate (mahmul) — this is in a categorical proposition (qadiyya hamliyya). If it is conditional (shartiyya), then they are called antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tali) respectively. And in the sought (matlub) and conclusion (natija), the subject is called the minor term (asghar) and the predicate the major term (akbar). And the proof (dalil) necessarily consists of two...
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage elaborates on how universal propositions (qadaya kulliyyah) from Usul al-Fiqh function in legal reasoning. The author explains that when a jurist constructs a categorical syllogism (e.g. in the first figure of qiyas hamli iftira'i), the major premise is a universal proposition from Usul al-Fiqh. Similarly, in a conditional syllogism (qiyas sharti istithna'i) based on universal implications, the conditional statement (mulazama) is also a universal qadiyya from Usul al-Fiqh. An example is given: 'Whenever qiyas indicates the establishment of a ruling, that ruling is established.' The text also addresses that these universal propositions may not be explicitly found in Usul al-Fiqh in the exact form used, but they are included implicitly in more general principles (through tadammun, inclusiveness). The commentator from al-Talwih then provides a classification of a complete compound proposition (murakkab tam) according to seven different aspects: as a proposition (qadiyya), report (khabar), informing (ikhbar), premise (muqaddima), sought (matlub), conclusion (natija), and problem (mas'ala). In a categorical proposition, the subject is called 'mawdu' and predicate 'mahmul'; in a conditional, they are 'muqaddam' and 'tali'. In the context of a proof (dalil), the subject of the sought/conclusion is the minor term (asghar) and the predicate the major term (akbar). This demonstrates the logical structure underlying Hanafi usul al-fiqh.

Key Terms
قضية كلية (qadiyya kulliyya): Universal proposition that serves as a major premise or conditional in qiyas
مركب تام (murakkab tam): Complete compound proposition that is either true or false
قضية حملیة (qadiyya hamliyya): Categorical proposition with subject and predicate
قضية شرطية (qadiyya shartiyya): Conditional proposition with antecedent and consequent

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a conditional proposition, the two parts are called:
A) Subject and predicate
B) Minor and major
C) Antecedent and consequent
D) Premise and conclusion

Key Takeaway
A complete compound proposition can be classified into seven names based on different aspects, and the terminology for its parts differs in categorical versus conditional propositions.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate
10 min
Validity of Analogy for a Known Specifier
usul dispute on ta'lil of known specifier

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi position on ta'lil of a known specifier and evaluate al-Jubba'i's opposing argument.

Core Idea
A known specifier is an independent text; its legal cause can be extended by analogy.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, a known specifier (mukhassis ma'loom) is considered a fully independent text (nass mustaqill), not a mere clarification like an exception. This lesson explores a key dispute: can the cause (illa) of such a specifier be used for analogical reasoning (qiyas)? The Hanafi school says yes, while al-Jubba'i (a prominent Mu'tazili) says no. Understanding this dispute sharpens your grasp of how independency of texts affects the scope of qiyas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two doubts
The passage introduces two possible ways to view a known specifier: resembling an abrogator (nasikh) or resembling an exception (istithna'). Your task is to see how each leads to a different ruling on ta'lil.
First doubt: specifier like nasikh → ta'lil valid. Second doubt: specifier like istithna' → ta'lil invalid.
2
State the Hanafi position
Hanafis hold that ta'lil of a known specifier is valid. The reason is that the specifier is an independent text (nass mustaqill) that can stand alone as a source of law, just like the original abrogated text.
Independence is the key: it can be used for qiyas without causing unrestricted abrogation.
3
State al-Jubba'i's objection
Al-Jubba'i argues that a specifier is like an exception (istithna') — it merely clarifies the scope of the general text and is not independent. Since non-inclusion (adam) cannot have a cause, ta'lil is invalid.
Exception analogy: istithna' is not an independent ruling, so no qiyas.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response clarifies that the specifier's independence allows ta'lil, but this does not mean the general is entirely abrogated. The analogy is limited to the specifier's own scope.
The first doubt (resembling nasikh) is about independence, not unrestricted abrogation.
5
Determine the practical consequence
If ta'lil is valid, we can extend the specifier's ruling to analogous cases via qiyas. If invalid, the specifier remains confined to its explicit individuals.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf: the difference in applicable rulings.

Source Text

متبادر یہ تھا کہ مخصص کو چونکہ مشابہت ہے بالناسخ ہے اس لئے اس کی تعلیل یعنی علت تخصیص کا بالقیاس پیش کرنا صحیح ہے جیسے ناسخ کی تعلیل قیاسی صحیح ہے جو بعض افراد عام کو منسوخ کرتا ہے اور پھر اس سے یہ بحیثیت نص مستقل ہونے کے تعلیل صحیح ہے خلاف للجبائی جہمی معتزلی کا اس میں خلاف ہے کہ وہ اس کی تعلیل صحیح قرار نہیں دیتا۔ فاذا صح تعلیلیہ جب اس مخصص کی تعلیل عندنا وعند اکثر العلماء صحیح ہوئی تو معلوم نہیں ہو گا کہ اس تعلیل یعنی قیاس کے ساتھ کتنا خارج ہیں اور کتنا تحت العام باقی ہیں تو اس لحاظ سے فیما بقی تحت العام جہالت واقع ہو جائیگی اور شبہ ثانی یعنی شبه استثناء کی بنا پر اس کی تعلیل صحیح نہیں ہے ۔ كما هو عند البعض اسی عند الجبائی وہ کہتا ہے کہ مخصص در حقیقت نص مستقل نہیں ہے بلکہ وہ بمنزلہ وصف کے ہے جو صدر کلام کے ساتھ قائم ہے جو اس پر دال ہے کہ یہ افراد صدر کلام کے حکم میں داخل نہیں ہیں تو یہ جب یہ عدم دخول پر دال ہوا تو والعدم لا یعلل کما هو فى الاستثناء ندخل الشك في سقوط العام کیونکہ شبہ اول کی بنا پر اس میں جہالت ہے لہذا عام کو ساقط ہو جانا چاہئے اور شبہ ثانی کی بنا پر سقوط نہیں ہونا چاہئے۔ مصنف نے اس کی وضاحت کرتے ہوئے کہا کہ شبہ ثانی یعنی شبه استثناء باعین حیثیت قائم ہے که مخصص بیان کرتا ہے کہ مخصوص افراد حکم عام میں داخل نہیں ہیں تو اس کی تعلیل صحیح نہیں ہے جیسے کہ جبائی کا مذہب ہے کہ جس طرح تعلیل مستثنی صحیح نہیں ہوا کرتی اس مخصص کی بھی صحیح نہیں ہے ۔
And if the specifier is known, i.e., if the specifier is known, covering known individuals. Because of the first resemblance, its ta'lil is correct. In the case of a known specifier, on the basis of the first doubt, its ta'lil is correct, because from the author's expression it was understood that the specifier, since it resembles the abrogator, therefore its ta'lil, i.e., presenting the cause of specification by analogy, is correct. Just as the analogical ta'lil of the abrogator is correct, which abrogates some individuals of the general, and then it would follow that with analogy other individuals of the general could also be abrogated, but this is not at all correct. So the ta'lil of the abrogator in this way is not valid: that by this analogical cause abrogation should continue in the future. This is not correct. Therefore the author, in his saying 'with what he intends' defended against this, saying that by the first doubt, he does not mean from the aspect that it resembles the abrogator. Rather, he means that the known specifier is a text that is independent in itself and conveys a ruling, so its ta'lil is correct. As is the case with us (Hanafis): an independent text's ta'lil is correct. According to us and most scholars, the ta'lil of a known specifier in its capacity as an independent text is correct, contrary to al-Jubba'i, the Jahmi Mu'tazili, who disagrees and does not consider its ta'lil correct. When its ta'lil is correct according to us and most scholars, then it will not be known how much is excluded by this ta'lil (qiyas) and how much remains under the general. From this aspect, ignorance occurs in what remains under the general. And due to the second doubt, i.e., the resemblance to exception, its ta'lil is not correct. As is the case with some, i.e., al-Jubba'i, who says that the specifier is in reality not an independent text, but rather it is like a description attached to the opening speech that indicates that these individuals do not enter under the ruling of the opening speech. So when it indicates non-inclusion, then non-inclusion is not subject to ta'lil, as in the case of exception. This introduces doubt about the dropping of the general. Because due to the first doubt there is ignorance in it, so the general should drop, and due to the second doubt, it should not drop. The author, clarifying this, said that the second doubt, i.e., the resemblance to exception, exists in the very capacity that the specifier states that the specified individuals are not included in the general ruling. So its ta'lil is not correct, just as al-Jubba'i's school holds that just as the ta'lil of the excepted is not correct, so the ta'lil of this specifier is also not correct.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute (ikhtilaf) about the validity of applying analogical reasoning (qiyas) to a known specifier (mukhassis ma'loom). The author distinguishes between two aspects of resemblance: first, resemblance to an abrogator (nasikh), which would allow ta'lil; second, resemblance to an exception (istithna'), which would not. The Hanafi position, shared by most scholars, is that a known specifier is an independent text (nass mustaqill), so its ta'lil is valid. Al-Jubba'i (a Mu'tazili scholar) argues that the specifier is not independent but merely describes the general, like an exception, and thus its ta'lil is invalid. The author defends the Hanafi view: although applying qiyas to the specifier may cause uncertainty about how much remains under the general, this uncertainty does not invalidate the specifier's authority. The passage resolves that the first doubt (resemblance to nasikh) is not about literal resemblance but about the specifier's independence, thus ta'lil is valid. The second doubt (resemblance to exception) is rejected because the specifier operates differently from an exception.

Key Terms
مخصص معلوم (mukhassis ma'loom): A known specifier with specified individuals.
تعلیل (ta'lil): Attributing a legal cause (illa) to a ruling to allow analogical extension.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view, why is ta'lil of a known specifier (mukhassis ma'loom) valid?
A) Because it resembles an exception (istithna').
B) Because it is an independent text (nass mustaqill) that can stand alone.
C) Because it abrogates the general text entirely.
D) Because it is a speculative proof (dalil zanni).

Key Takeaway
The validity of ta'lil for a known specifier depends on whether it is considered an independent text (Hanafi) or mere description (al-Jubba'i).

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning

Competing Analogies in Conditional Sales: Naskh vs. Istithna
Usul analysis of competing qiyas in conditional sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi method of tarjih between competing qiyas (shabah naskh vs. shabah istithna) to determine the validity of a sale with a khiyar condition on part of the merchandise.

Core Idea
When a sale includes a khiyar on part of the item, validity hinges on which analogy outweighs — abrogation (naskh) or exception (istithna).

This passage delves into a nuanced usul problem: how to rule when a seller offers two items together but grants khiyar (option) only on one. Two conflicting analogies arise: shabah naskh (resembling abrogation, where the void part is removed and the rest stands) and shabah istithna (resembling a void condition, which invalidates the whole). The Hanafi school resolves this by weighing the circumstances — specifically, whether the locus of khiyar and the price are known. Understanding this tarjih mechanism is key to mastering how Hanafi jurists prioritize competing legal analogies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two competing analogies
Read the passage and locate the two shabahs (analogies): shabah naskh (abrogation) and shabah istithna (exception). Understand how each analogy would apply to a sale where part of the merchandise is subject to a condition.
Naskh analogy treats the condition as removable, leaving the rest valid. Istithna analogy treats the condition as a void exception, invalidating the entire sale.
2
Map the four scenarios
List the four scenarios: (1) both khiyar and price known; (2) price unknown; (3) khiyar unknown; (4) both unknown. For each, consider how each analogy would rule.
The passage explicitly labels these scenarios (al-thani, al-thalith, al-rabi).
3
Understand the tarjih (preference) mechanism
The passage states that when both khiyar and price are known, shabah naskh is preferred, making the sale valid. When either is unknown, shabah istithna prevails, making it invalid. This preference is not arbitrary but based on the presence of knowledge (ma'lumiyya) tipping the scale toward validity.
Knowledge of the condition and price removes the uncertainty that would trigger the istithna analogy.
4
Apply the tarjih to all four scenarios
Work through each scenario: (1) both known -> valid; (2) price unknown -> invalid (ignorance of price triggers istithna); (3) khiyar unknown -> invalid; (4) both unknown -> invalid.
The common void condition (accepting non-sold item for sold item) exists in all scenarios, but knowledge overrides it in scenario 1.
5
Contrast with the free man/slave case
The passage ends with a contrasting case: selling a free man and a slave together. Here, the free man cannot be a valid object of sale, so only shabah istithna applies; the sale of the slave is invalid according to Abu Hanifa. This shows when one analogy is exclusive.
This case reinforces that shabah naskh requires the condition to be potentially removable from a valid contract; the free man is fundamentally outside commerce.

In it there is khiyar but the price is not known, how much is for which. The third scenario is the opposite of the second: the locus of khiyar is not known but the price is known, that this one is one thousand and that one is also one thousand, but it is not known whether the khiyar is in that one or in this one. The fourth scenario is that neither the locus of khiyar nor the price is known, e.g., he sold both for two thousand on the condition that he has khiyar in one of them. So we considered whether it is included: if we consider it as included in the offer, and it is an instance of shabah naskh, then in all four scenarios the sale is valid. At most, this defect occurs: that the sale becomes partial (bay' bi al-hissa), but this partiality is not from the beginning but persists, because both slaves entered the offer and the sold item is in one sale, so initially there was no partiality, but when one is rejected due to the khiyar condition, then partiality occurs in persistence, and the sale is not invalid. And if we consider it as not included in the ruling of the sale, then in all four scenarios the sale is invalid. As for when both are known: the reason for invalidity is that when the locus of khiyar is not included in the ruling, it is as if it is not sold, so acceptance of the non-sold item becomes a condition for acceptance of the sold item. Since the offer is one, the buyer cannot accept only one; he accepts both. So when acceptance of the non-sold item is conditioned for acceptance of the sold item, this is a void condition. And when one of the locus or price is unknown, like the second scenario where only the price is unknown and the third where only the locus is unknown, then in the second both the void condition and ignorance of price exist, and in the third the sold item is unknown and the void condition exists. In the fourth, both price and sold item are unknown, and the void condition exists because the sold item is one, the condition of accepting the non-sold item for acceptance of the sold item exists in all. So besides this common cause of invalidity, in the second there is ignorance of price, in the third ignorance of the sold item, in the fourth ignorance of both. When it is known that the aspect of similarity to naskh is the instrument, then due to shabah naskh the sale should be valid in all four scenarios, because after abrogation the ruling on the remainder remains valid. And shabah istithna causes invalidity because the void condition exists. So we considered both analogies: when the locus of khiyar or price or both are unknown, the sale is declared invalid due to shabah istithna; and when both the locus and price are known, then the validity is given due to shabah naskh, and shabah istithna is not considered, because the knowledge of the locus and price gives preference to the side of validity. Contrast the case when one sells a free man and a slave together for one thousand and specifies the price for each separately: then the sale of the slave is invalid according to Abu Hanifa, because the free man cannot be included in the sale, so this is only shabah istithna, not shabah naskh. So acceptance of the non-sold item is a condition for acceptance of the sold item, and this is a void condition, thus the sale is invalid.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage is a detailed juristic analysis of a sale contract involving two slaves where the buyer has the condition of khiyar (option) in one of them, but the price is set for the whole. The author examines four scenarios: (1) both khiyar and price known, (2) price unknown, (3) khiyar unknown, (4) both unknown. The ruling hinges on two competing analogies: shabah bi al-naskh (resemblance to abrogation, where the void part is canceled and the rest remains valid) and shabah bi al-istithna (resemblance to an exception, which void the contract). If we view the khiyar condition as part of the offer (dakhil fi al-ijab), the analogy to naskh favors validity, with only the defect of partiality (taba'ud) arising, which does not invalidate the sale. If we view it as not part of the ruling, all scenarios are invalid due to a void condition (accepting the non-sold item for the acceptance of the sold item). The preferred view is to weigh both shabahs: when both khiyar and price are known, shabah naskh is given priority, making the sale valid; when either is unknown, shabah istithna prevails, making it invalid. At the end, the passage contrasts the case of selling a free man and a slave together, where the free man cannot be a valid object of sale, so the analogy is solely istithna, leading to invalidity per Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms
خیار (khiyar): Option or condition to rescind a sale within a specified period.
شبه (shabah): Analogical resemblance, a basis for istihsan.
نسخ (naskh): Abrogation; here used as analogy for removing part of contract.
استثناء (istithna): Exception; here analogy for a condition that voids the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because of shabah naskh
B) Because of shabah istithna and ignorance of the khiyar locus
C) Because the price is known
D) Because the sale is partial

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, when competing analogies (shabah naskh and shabah istithna) conflict, the ruling is determined by tarjih based on knowledge of the condition's locus and price; certainty favors validity, ignorance favors invalidity.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning

Shabah al-Nasikh and Shabah al-Istithna: Weighing Conflicting Semblances in Sale
Analyzes how conflicting semblances determine validity of a sale with conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two types of legal semblance (shabah) — shabah al-nasikh and shabah al-istithna — and apply the Hanafi rule for weighing them in a sale with conditions.

Core Idea
When a condition casts both a preserving and corrupting semblance on a sale, knowledge of the price and option subject tilts the balance toward validity.

This passage explores a nuanced Hanafi usul problem: when a sale contains a condition that generates conflicting legal semblances, how does the jurist determine whether the sale is valid or corrupt? The answer lies in weighing two analogical semblances — one that preserves validity (shabah al-nasikh) and one that corrupts (shabah al-istithna). Let us examine the rule and its application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two semblances
Recognize shabah al-nasikh (semblance of abrogation) which leaves the remainder of the contract valid, and shabah al-istithna (semblance of exception) which corrupts the contract due to an invalid condition.
These are not actual abrogation/exception but legal analogies.
2
Determine the source of conflict
Both semblances arise from the same condition. The condition partially resembles abrogation (removing part of the contract is valid) and partially resembles an exception (attaching an invalid condition corrupts).
3
Apply the decisive factor: knowledge of terms
When the subject of the option (khiyar) and the price are known, shabah al-nasikh prevails and the sale is valid. When either is unknown, shabah al-istithna prevails and the sale is corrupt.
Knowledge gives priority to validity.
4
Contrast with the free-person-with-slave case
When a free person is sold with a slave (free person cannot be sold), only shabah al-istithna applies, so the sale is corrupt even if price is known.
This shows that shabah al-nasikh does not apply when the condition cannot be analogous to abrogation.
5
Reflect on the talīl methodology
The Hanafi approach uses careful weighing of conflicting analogies (shabah) to reach a ruling, prioritising knowledge and feasibility.
This is a form of tarjīh al-ashbāh (preference among semblances).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبه بالفسيخ آلہ تو شبہ بالنسخ کی وجہ سے بیع چاروں صورتوں میں صحیح ہونی چاہتے کیوں کہ ناسخ کے بعد مابقی میں حکم صحیح رہتا ہے وشبه الاستثنا صحیح رہتا ہے وشبه الاستثناء يوجب الفساد فى الـ کیونکہ شرط فاسد موجود ہے ۔ فراعينا الشبہین تو ہم نے دونوں شھیں کا اعتبار کیا تو جب محل خیار یا من یا دونوں مجہول ہیں تو شبہ استثناء کی وجہ سے بیع کو فاسد قرار دیا گیا اور جب محل خیارا در شمن ہر ایک معلوم ہے تو اس صورت میں شبہ ناسخ کا اعتبار کر کے بیع کو صحیح قرار دیا اور اس صورت میں شہر استثناء کا اعتبار نہیں کیا گیا کہ شرط فاسد کا اعتبار کر کے یعنی ماتيس بمبیع کا قبول شرط برائے قبول مبیع کی وجہ سے فاسد کہا جاتا یوں نہیں کیا گیا کیونکہ محل خیار اور ثمن کی معلومیہ حانب صحت کو ترجیح دیتی ہے بخلاف ما اذا باغ الحرد العبد بخلاف اس صورت کے جب حر اور عہد کو ایک ہزار کے ساتھ فى صفقة واحدة فروخت کرتا ہے اور ہر ایک کا شمن نبھی الگ الگ بیان کر دیتا ہے تو اس میں بیع فاسد ہو جائیگی عبد میں بھی عند ابی حنیفہ بان الحر کیونکہ ربیع میں داخل ہی نہیں ہوسکتا لہذا یہ صرف مشابه بالاستثنام ہو گا مشابه بالنسخ نہیں ہوگا تو مالیں لمبیع کی قبولیت شرط ہو گی لقبول المبیع اور یہ شرط فاسد ہے لہذا بیع فاسد ہوگی
The semblance of abrogation (shabah al-nasikh) leads to the sale being valid in all four cases, because after abrogation the ruling remains valid for what remains. And the semblance of exception (shabah al-istithna) leads to corruption because an invalid condition exists. We considered both semblances: when the subject of the option (khiyar) or price or both are unknown, the sale is ruled corrupt due to the semblance of exception. When the subject of the option and the price are each known, in that case the semblance of abrogation is considered and the sale is ruled valid, and the semblance of exception is not considered – i.e. the invalid condition is not given weight by saying that acceptance of what is not sold is a condition for acceptance of the sold item, causing corruption – because the knowledge of the subject of option and the price gives priority to validity. Unlike the case when he sells a free person and a slave for one thousand in a single transaction and specifies the price for each separately – there the sale becomes corrupt in the slave as well according to Abu Hanifa, because the free person cannot be included in the sale, so this is merely shabah al-istithna and not shabah al-nasikh. Thus acceptance of what is not sold becomes a condition for acceptance of the sold item, and this is an invalid condition, so the sale is corrupt.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of a previous rule about the interaction of two types of legal semblance (shabah) in sales contracts. The commentator explains that when a sale includes a condition that creates both a semblance of abrogation (shabah al-nasikh) and a semblance of exception (shabah al-istithna), the ruling depends on whether the key terms are known or unknown. If the price or the subject of option is unknown, the corrupting effect of the exception semblance dominates and the sale is void. If both are known, the preserving effect of the abrogation semblance dominates and the sale is valid. Then he contrasts a case where a free person is sold together with a slave: here only the semblance of exception applies (since the free person cannot be sold), so the condition is invalid and the sale is corrupt. This analysis follows the Hanafi methodology of balancing conflicting analogies.

Key Terms
شبه بالنسخ (shabah al-nasikh): A legal semblance where the condition is seen as abrogating part of the contract, leaving the rest valid.
شبه بالاستثناء (shabah al-istithna): A legal semblance where the condition is like an exception, causing corruption because it is invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does shabah al-nasikh take precedence over shabah al-istithna in a sale?
A) When the price is unknown
B) When the subject of option and the price are both known
C) When a free person is sold with a slave
D) When the condition is invalid

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, conflicting legal semblances in a sale are resolved by prioritizing validity (shabah al-nasikh) when the condition's terms are known, otherwise corruption (shabah al-istithna) prevails.

Extending the Prophet's Act by Qiyas: Fard Prayer Inside the Kaaba
Hanafi response to Shafi'i objection on fard prayer inside Kaaba

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi qiyas jaliy argument extending the permissibility of nafl prayer inside the Kaaba to fard prayer.

Core Idea
When the 'illa is shared, the Prophet's specific act becomes a precedent for all similar acts.

This lesson examines a classic usul dispute between the Hanafis and Imam al-Shafi'i. The question: does the Prophet's (peace be upon him) supererogatory prayer inside the Kaaba allow obligatory prayer there too? The Hanafis answer yes through qiyas. You will trace the reasoning and see how Hanafi usul employs analogical extension from a proven act.

Guided Path
1
Identify the positions
First, state the Shafi'i position: fard prayer inside Kaaba is invalid because the Prophet's act was only nafl and perfect qibla facing is impossible. Then state the Hanafi position: both fard and nafl are allowed.
Use the source text to extract each claim.
2
Isolate the Shafi'i dalil and its basis
Al-Shafi'i relies on the nature of the Prophet's act (fi'l) being limited to nafl. He also argues that fard requires complete facing of the Kaaba, which is impossible inside.
Notice the Shafi'i restriction of fi'l to its specific context.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response with qiyas
The Hanafis claim that the 'illa (effective cause) for the permissibility of nafl inside the Kaaba is facing the qibla in the state of ikhtiyar (ability). This same 'illa exists for fard because both require facing the Kaaba. Hence, through qiyas jaliy, fard is also permitted.
Qiyas jaliy: the 'illa is manifest and undisputed.
4
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: in the Hanafi school, one may perform any prayer (fard, wajib, nafl) inside the Kaaba. In the Shafi'i school, only nafl is permitted; fard must be performed outside.
This difference affects pilgrims and those visiting the Kaaba.
5
Reflect on the usul principle
This case illustrates how Hanafis apply qiyas to extend a ruling from a specific prophetic act. It contrasts with the Shafi'i approach which restricts the act to its immediate context.
Consider how each school's usul methodology leads to different rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الشافعي لايجوز الفرض في الكعبه امام شافعی کے نزدیک نماز فرضیہ کعبہ کے اندر جائز نہیں ہے کیونکہ بعض اجزائے کعبہ پس پشت ہو جائیں گے تو استقبال کعبہ پوری طرح نہ ہوا اس لئے فریضہ جائز نہیں ہے اور رسول اللہ صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم کا فعل محمول علی النفل ہے تو صرف نفل نماز جائز ہوگی۔ ونحن نقول ہم اس کے جواب میں کہتے جب بعض کا جواز یعنی نفل کا جواز بفعل النبی علیہ السلام ثابت ہوا تو فرائض کا جواز قیاس سے ثابت ہو جائیگا کیونکہ فرائض اور نوافل میں بحالت اختیار استقبال کعبہ میں تساوی ثابت ہے یعنی دونوں کے اندر استقبال کعبہ ضروری ہے تو فرائض کا جواز فی الکعبہ ثابت بالقیاس ہوگا۔ لہذا ہر قسم کی صلوۃ خواہ فرض ہو یا نقل کعبہ کے اندر جائز ہے۔
Imam al-Shafi'i said: Obligatory prayer inside the Kaaba is not permissible. Because some parts of the Kaaba will be behind one's back, so facing the Kaaba is not completely achieved; therefore, obligatory prayer is not permissible. And the act of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) is interpreted as supererogatory; thus only supererogatory prayer is permissible. And we say in response: When the permissibility of some, i.e., the permissibility of supererogatory prayer, is proven by the act of the Prophet (peace be upon him), then the permissibility of obligatory prayer is proven by analogy, because in the state of ability, facing the Kaaba is equal between obligatory and supererogatory prayers, i.e., facing the Kaaba is necessary in both. Therefore, the permissibility of obligatory prayer inside the Kaaba is proven by analogy. Hence every type of prayer, whether obligatory or supererogatory, is permissible inside the Kaaba.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage presents a usul dispute between the Hanafis and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding the ruling of performing obligatory prayer inside the Kaaba. Al-Shafi'i argues that because the Prophet's reported prayer inside the Kaaba was supererogatory (nafl), it does not prove the permissibility of fard. He insists that fard requires complete facing of the Kaaba, which is impossible inside it. The Hanafi response uses qiyas: since nafl is proven by the Prophet's act (fi'l), and both nafl and fard share the same 'illa (facing the Kaaba in the state of ability), fard is established by analogy. Thus all prayers, fard and nafl, are permitted inside the Kaaba. This showcases the Hanafi method of extending a ruling from a proven case (nafl) to an unproven one (fard) through qiyas jaliy (clear analogy).

Key Terms
فعل النبي (fi'l al-nabi): The actual deed of the Prophet (peace be upon him) which can indicate a ruling but not general legislativity unless accompanied by a qarinah.
قياس جلي (qiyas jaliy): An analogy where the 'illa is clearly present in both the original and the branch.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did Hanafis allow fard prayer inside the Kaaba despite the Prophet's act being nafl?
A) Because the Prophet said so explicitly.
B) Because fard and nafl share the 'illa of facing the qibla.
C) Because the Kaaba is a mosque for all prayers.
D) Because Shafi'i agreed with them.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, when a prophetic act shares an 'illa with another act, the ruling can be extended through qiyas jaliy.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
advanced
10 min
The Invalidity of Transferring a Qualification by Qiyas
Refutation of transferring the condition of faith from one expiation to another via qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on transferring a qualification by qiyas and evaluate the opposing argument based on the conditions of qiyas and the nature of absolute texts.

Core Idea
A qualification cannot be transferred by qiyas if it leads to tahsil al-hasil, transfers a pure negation, or contradicts an absolute text.

In this lesson, we examine a classic usul dispute: can a qualification (qayd) from one ruling be transferred to another via qiyas? The case involves the condition of faith in the expiation for killing and its potential transfer to the expiation for an oath. The Hanafi school argues forcefully against this, revealing key principles about absolute texts and the limits of analogical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
State the opponent's claim
Identify what Imam al-Razi and earlier opponents argue: that the qualification of faith is a legal ruling established by text, so it can be transferred by qiyas to kaffarat al-yamin, with the negation of a non-believing slave's acceptance following incidentally.
The opponent relies on the permissibility of transferring a hukm shar'i via qiyas.
2
Analyze the first Hanafi refutation: Tahsil al-Hasil
The absolute text 'tahriru raqaba' in kaffarat al-yamin already covers believing slaves, so transferring the qualification obtains what is already obtained, which is futile (tahsil al-hasil).
This shows that qiyas cannot add a restriction when the absolute text already includes the qualified case.
3
Analyze the second refutation: Transfer of 'Adam
Transferring the qualification necessarily transfers the negation of acceptance for non-believing slaves, which is a pure negation ('adam asli). Qiyas operates only on positive legal rulings (ahkam), not on pure negations.
Even if the negation is incidental, the main purpose becomes transferring the negation, which is invalid.
4
Analyze the third refutation: Text in the Maqis
One condition of valid qiyas is that no text exists in the maqis that indicates the ruling. Here, the absolute text in kaffarat al-yamin indicates that any slave suffices, so qiyas is invalid because it would contradict this text.
Qiyas cannot override an existing text in the analogous case.
5
Examine the response to the specification argument
The opponent analogizes to specification of the general by qiyas, arguing that just as general can be specified, absolute can be qualified by qiyas. The Hanafi response: specification of general by qiyas is not absolutely permitted; it is restricted, so the analogy fails.
This refutes the opponent's attempt to justify carrying absolute to qualified via qiyas.

جواب ہے اس دلیل کا جو امام رازی نے محصول میں پیش فرمائی ہے اس امر یہ کہ حمل مطلق على المقید جائز ہے جب قیاس اس کا مقتضی ہو وھو ان الخ اور وہ دلیل یہ ہے کہ عام کی دلالہ علی الافراد فائق ہے اس دلالہ سے
